Readers' Club

Review of "A True Man" 234

— Yijun

Written by Fan Wenlan, Published by Kaiming Bookstore, 4 jiao

At a time when the call for "National Defense Literature" is resounding loudly, the publication of this book, "A True Man," can be said to be timely. From its title, we can guess that it must be about stories of national heroes. When we open the table of contents, this guess is confirmed without any discount. The book contains the stories of twenty-five national heroes in history, whose names are: Zhang Qian, Wei Qing, Huo Qubing, Li Guang, Su Wu, Zhao Chongguo, Ma Yuan, Ban Chao, Liu Kun, Xuanzang, Yan Gaoqing, Zhang Xun, Xu Yuan, Di Qing, Zong Ze, Yue Fei, Zhang Shijie, Lu Xiufu, Wen Tianxiang, Fang Xiaoru, Qi Jiguang, Xiong Tingbi, Yuan Chonghuan, Shi Kefa, and Huang Daozhou.

The author's motivation for writing this book is worthy of our praise. In the first item of the "Explanatory Notes," he says: "Mencius said: 'Wealth and rank cannot corrupt him, poverty and meanness cannot move him, might and force cannot subdue him. These are the qualities of a true man.' ... If a person has such firm self-confidence and such noble aspirations, I believe that any great and immortal cause and honor can be easily achieved. Therefore, this book is named 'A True Man,' and I hope that every reader will also learn to be a true man." At present, the trend of the world is frivolous and servile to the extreme, and there are even people who "only know how to serve foreigners, but forget the great righteousness between races." If we can put forward a few historical figures with both virtue and righteousness to serve as a warning to the public, wouldn't it be a very meaningful thing?

The author's criteria for selecting the ancients are also agreeable to me. He says, "...both morality and career are emphasized, with greater emphasis on morality. This is because the success or failure of a career is mostly governed by the environment, while the responsibility of morality can be shouldered by anyone. ...Do what is right, even at the cost of one's life. The more one knows that it cannot be done and yet does it, the more one shows the greatness of one's personality." Of course, there are not only these twenty-five such historical figures. In the five thousand years of history, there have been countless patriots who have sacrificed their lives to protect the lives of the nation. However, the deeds of these people have mostly been erased by later historians, and now we have no way to fabricate them. Therefore, the twenty-five people in this book actually represent the full personality of many national heroes in history.

The eras to which these twenty-five people belonged include the Western Han, Eastern Han, Eastern Jin, Tang, Northern Song, Southern Song, and Ming dynasties, and half of them are loyal officials and men of integrity in the late years of the dynasties, who are truly the true men who "know that it cannot be done and yet do it." Generally, at the end of a dynasty, society must be immersed in decadence, most people are living a life of drunken stupor in which they "get through one day at a time," and the political order must be extremely chaotic. Treacherous ministers gain power, and loyal and upright ministers suffer. At this time, if someone steps forward, speaks out for justice, and supports the precarious situation, the public's vision will be renewed, and the sense of respect and admiration will arise spontaneously. Although nine out of ten of their actions end in failure, "justice resides in the hearts of the people," and everyone with a little conscience will always remember them. Although official documents can erase their great achievements, they still cannot prevent the spread of folk tales, unofficial histories, notes, romances, and ballads from passing on their deeds to immortality. The most prominent example is Yue Fei. In official history, Yue Fei is only a person who advocated war, and his battles were not always beneficial. When we read the History of the Song Dynasty, we often feel that in such a difficult time for the Song Dynasty, Qin Hui's policy of peace was not so absurd, and that he, Han Shizhong, and their subordinates were all arrogant and brave soldiers who did not obey the orders of the central government. Summoning them back for questioning and punishment became a necessary means for the central government to rectify its authority. However, this attempt to cover up the eyes and ears of the world ultimately failed. We still have many unofficial histories and romances, and Yue Fei, who has existed in people's hearts for hundreds of years, is Yue Fei in the romances, a national hero with "righteousness reaching the heavens," and a true man with loyalty, filial piety, and integrity. There are many such cases. If we can restore everyone to their original appearance, it would be a very meaningful task.

The reason why Mr. Fan wrote this book about true men in history is better said to be that he was moved by the affairs of the time and used history to express his feelings and to warn us. After narrating the deeds of each person, he must attach a small commentary, which is the author's true intention. In the commentary after the article "Wei Qing," he says:

"...Originally, when a nation is oppressed and plundered by another nation, it is unquestionable that it must resist and take revenge... To live in peace and steal life, to dry one's face with saliva, and even to try to destroy one's own inherent strength as much as possible and be a vanguard for the enemy, such a slavish nation will certainly not be tolerated between heaven and earth!"

After the section "Zong Ze," he says:

"At the end of the Northern Song Dynasty, there were really many talents who could save the country, and their loyalty and integrity were unprecedented in the Han and Tang Dynasties... Unfortunately, the imperial court never believed that the Chinese people would have the ability to resist the Jin Dynasty, let alone believe that they would ultimately succeed. Therefore, the imperial court never dared to risk trusting people like Zong Ze and Yue Fei, who had made definite achievements, for fear of an irreversible defeat. The key to Emperor Suzong of Tang's ability to restore the old order was not running to Sichuan. Prince Kang lacked this spirit and only wanted to compromise and survive, but in fact, if it were not for Zong Ze and Yue Fei's desperate resistance, the partial peace would probably not have been established!... Therefore, we know that a country or a person who loses self-confidence in a crisis is truly terminally ill."

After the section "Zhang Shijie, Lu Xiufu," he says:

"This dynasty, which has been established for three hundred years, has always adopted the most peaceful and humble attitude towards foreign countries. According to reason, foreign countries should be at peace and should no longer advance an inch. Unfortunately, the Liao, Jin, and Yuan Dynasties did not understand those principles of courtesy and righteousness and only plundered forward. They really let down the Song Dynasty's good intentions! Emperor Taizong of Song failed in his Northern Expedition, and the Northern Song Dynasty never dared to dream of the Sixteen Prefectures of Youyun again. Emperor Gaozong of Song fled to Jiangnan, and the Southern Song Dynasty, the emperor and his ministers were content to survive in a small court and dared not look at the Central Plains again. When Lin'an fell and Deyou was captured, wouldn't it be unreasonable for the two or three loyal ministers to want to survive in the desolate and miasmic land and continue the last breath of the Zhao family? Finally, they drifted to the sea and dared not live on land again. Wouldn't this be even more unreasonable? However, the enemy does not care whether you are reasonable or not, and in short, they must be completely wiped out. Therefore, after reading the history of the Song Dynasty's demise, we know that if a country does not work hard and retreats from foreign countries, even a fool will understand its fate."

These words are so poignant and powerfully whipping at the hearts of each of us now! We will naturally understand the author's heart. I believe that everyone who reads these passages will think of the current affairs because of the lessons of history, and think that our current situation is precisely entering the last few years of the Song Dynasty!

Although this book only retells some historical events, we already feel that the author's intention is worthy of admiration.

Unfortunately, the author cannot write it in a literary style. If it could be written in the form of a historical novel, its effect would be ten or a hundred times greater than it is now. The author is very拘泥to the source of history. He says, "There is no word without origin, and no word without basis... Absolutely avoid the novelist's fabricated falsehoods and the ills of confusing facts." He thinks this is the advantage of the book, but we can only think it is the shortcoming of the book. We may not discuss whether historical novels (that is, the romances that the author said) are the same as the author's understanding, even if they are fabricated falsehoods (in fact, it is definitely not so), it is not inconsistent with the author's motivation for writing this book. Doesn't the author's "great righteousness" also have to use historical facts to warn our current people? What we ask of the readers is not only "to know a little history without digesting it, but more importantly, to obtain serious lessons from it." In this way, listing facts is obviously worse than describing facts. We hope that someone else will take on this task. Mr. Fan has already collected the most detailed raw materials for us here.

Editor's Note 235

Recently, Kaiming Bookstore has published several history and geography books aimed at this great era, which are very much needed by young people, such as: "A True Man," "The Story of National Crisis," "Shu Road," etc. Readers are very excited about these books. We can tell from the many reviews and reflections we have received on these books. This journal published Mr. He Si's "Where Should We Go?" in the last issue. This issue published Mr. Yijun's "Review of A True Man" and Mr. Jia Ming's "Postscript to Reading Shu Road," which are very useful for those who plan to read these books.

Postscript to Reading "Shu Road" 236

— Jia Ming

Since the unification of Sichuan Province and the restoration of security, due to the increasingly serious national crisis, people across the country have gained a new understanding and developed a new hope for the future of Sichuan Province. Therefore, the province has gradually become a target of national attention.

In the past year or two, there has been a considerable flurry of documents about the exploration of Sichuan, from complete masterpieces to scattered essays, scattered in newspapers, magazines, and printed publications. However, from a certain standpoint, I have a special sympathy for this book and consider it to be a fairly accurate account.

In general, Sichuan is nothing more than a place with abundant products and beautiful scenery. The concept of "Land of Abundance" will not be blurred in the consciousness of any Chinese person. The beauty of the Three Gorges and the Jialing River is always the content of poets' imaginations! However, in this book, there is a further, realistic discovery, which makes any Chinese person, after receiving it, unable to help but feel surprised and awakened in addition to envy and fantasy.

According to the author's observations, Sichuan is indeed "rich" and "beautiful," but if we further investigate the lives of the people, there is another strange word—"miserable." In this small jade country, the rich are rich and the poor are poor. There are abundant materials, but people don't know how to use them. There is beautiful scenery, but it is only for a part of the privileged class to fully enjoy. Even under the greatest possible invasion and exploitation, the powerless are unable to live, and as a result, huge numbers are manifested in the mortality rate, to the point where women have no clothes and pants to cover their shame, and the starving corpses fill the roads and people eat each other.

Based on the author's hearsay and eyewitness accounts, we can appreciate the following abandoned writings of the "miserable" world with an emotion of "believing that there is no such thing":

"Chen, a resident of Banbian Street in Songpan, committed suicide by eating his eight-year-old biological daughter, and soon the woman also died of illness and starvation. Within hundreds of miles along the way, human blood and bones and those who died of starvation filled the ditches, which was unbearable to witness."

"On March 1, it was heard that four of the seven members of a family in Caojiagou had already starved to death, and the remaining three were also dying... Many famine refugees from afar passed through the area and were all killed... They were divided, roasted, and eaten without residue..."

In history, there have often been tragic situations of "exchanging children for food and cooking skeletons for cooking" in the country and city. In this generation, they can actually eat their own children "without exchanging," which makes the former pale in comparison! Another example is a road worker who complained to the author, "...We are all poor people, working one day and eating one day. During the busy farming season, we are not allowed to farm, but we are forced to work. We are not given money or food. Where do we have surplus grain? We borrowed a few liters of rice from our neighbors, carried it, and walked sixty miles, and then we walked here. Now the rice is gone, and the work is not finished. We are going to starve to death here..." This is probably an even rarer "miracle"! We know that the distribution of workers' rations and the distance between the residence of those who are requisitioned and the work site are all regulated by central laws, right? Why is there such a factual discovery? I think that in addition to the author's shrewdness and courage, it is probably not something that ordinary articles and books can discover.

In this province, there is also a special phenomenon of oppression, which is the so-called field tax problem that many people have reported on. This book also contains profound and detailed records, as follows:

"Taking one hundred mu as a unit... In the early years of the Republic of China, the tax was changed to sixteen yuan. During the defense zone era, the tax was increased to thirty yuan. This is for one year, also known as one tax. If one year is taxed for six years, then 180 yuan is required; if it is taxed for ten years, then 300 yuan is required; if one month is taxed for one year, then 360 yuan is required... Those with special power have more land and less grain; otherwise, they have less land and more grain. There are those who pay grain every year but do not know where the land is, which is called flying grain... So how much grain should be paid for how much land is a question that even the landlords cannot answer... In areas such as Baxian, it is calculated in "dan"; in areas such as Yongchuan, it is calculated in "tiao"..."

"In 1923, Chengdu County had already collected taxes until 1979... It is said that the most is Guangyuan County, which collected taxes until 2001..."

"At present, the burden on the people of Sichuan Province, in terms of field tax, although the provincial government has done its best to rectify it... still requires the collection of grain for seven or eight years, or even more than ten years in one year..."

Under this heavy burden, what is the way out for the people? Those who are strong and sturdy will have to take risks,揭扜and rise up, and there will be so-called "sectarian bandits" and "local bandits." The weak and incompetent can only fall into the situation of "death" and "digging corpses." As the author said, "Those who can be tempted by bandits today may not be able to be used by the enemy tomorrow... The worries of the overall national situation are tied to this, and it should not be taken lightly."

In addition to these exposures of the real world, the author uses his clear and simple literary language to describe many beautiful scenes. However, the author always stands on the position of education and social service, and all descriptions are taken in a common-sense and reporting manner. Therefore, this book is completely different from ordinary pure literary travel notes, and it is suitable for people who need rich common sense and experiences.

In addition, the author claims that there are many purposes for going to Sichuan, among which there is also the so-called: "I want to see what the thoughts and abilities of the general young people in Sichuan are like? How will they cultivate themselves to shoulder future responsibilities?" Based on this, lectures to young male and female students are agreed to as long as time permits. A total of thirty-four lectures have been given, covering areas such as saving the country, starting a career, and marriage. It is said that several times, many young people were moved to tears. At the same time, the author often made appointments with many young people to talk alone. There were not enough seats in the residence, so they often talked on the ground. Therefore, this book can be read not only as a travelogue but also as a book on youth cultivation.

The whole book is divided into five sections. The first section is the concept of traveling to Sichuan. The second section is a hundred-day diary of traveling to Sichuan, which is the main part of the book. The descriptions of social life and natural scenery are all included in this book. The third section is a letter to young students in Sichuan, which I believe can provide considerable help for the cultivation of young people in general and is by no means something that "only young people in Sichuan are worth reading." For example, what is said: "The national crisis is forcing us to this point, can we still study to death?... Everyone, open your eyes wide!" Aren't these very broad and timely? The fourth section is a brief overview of the transformation of new Sichuan. It is said that the author named the book "Shu Road" for two reasons: ㈠ The "Shu Road" of "Shu Road is difficult." ㈡ The way to govern Shu, this section is aimed at the latter. The fifth section is a hundred quatrains of traveling to Shu. This new and refreshing poetic style is also worth reading for friends who generally love old poems.

Reading "Bacteria and Humans" 237

— Dong Lin

Written by Gao Shiqi, Published by Kaiming Bookstore, 4 jiao 5 fen

"Regarding bacteriology, I... will gradually use simple and interesting words to disguise this mysterious and subtle science, no, to strip it naked, so that it becomes not a strange commodity for experts, but a snack and supplement for the general public..." It is said on page 121 of the original book that the author of "Bacteria and Humans" describes a passage in which he writes scientific essays. After reading this collection, I do feel that the author has achieved this point.

Science is the most practical and most needed kind of knowledge in life. If mankind wants to conquer nature, they must consult Mr. Science.

Ordinary science readings are like moralistic gentlemen, making people respect and fear them. The average person does not dare to ask him for advice.

If someone were to dress up scientific articles in literary attire and meet the public, I think the public would be very happy to approach him and ask him for advice.

The collection of scientific essays, "Bacteria and Humans," is a practical example before us. (I heard that many people welcome the author's articles.)

This booklet is a collection of twenty-nine scientific essays, most of which are about bacteriology. Bacteriology is originally a specialized field of study. Books on bacteria written by specialists are probably dry and tasteless professional books, which are not welcomed by the general public. However, the author used light-hearted essays to write the common sense of this science, which makes people read with relish and does not feel boring. This is very gratifying.

The author can be said to be very successful in writing scientific essays. His success is due to his rare talent.

First, he can write dry and tasteless scientific knowledge in a very interesting way. Some places simply have a lot of humor, which makes readers smile at the corners of their mouths when they see it.

Second, the author can cleverly mix and describe scientific knowledge with human affairs, and can naturally connect the two sides without feeling stiff. He often takes the opportunity to express his views and satirize the ugliness of the world. Originally cold and rational scientific articles have been transformed into very lively essays with emotions under the author's pen. This cannot but be said to be the author's success.

Third, the author sometimes uses a historical writing style to write about the historical facts of scientists fighting bacteria, inspiring readers on how mankind should strive to fight against nature in order to obtain their own happiness. This is also a good writing style.

Fourth, the author's writing is very fluent, which is of course also one of the elements of success.

The author is successful in writing skills. Sometimes, perhaps because the author pays too much attention to the effort in skills, a few articles are inevitably a little insufficient in content. For example, in the article about color blindness, I feel that the reason for color blindness is not written adequately. I think that when writing scientific essays, you should not only write about superficial phenomena, but also explain its essence. The author not only wants readers to know a certain state of affairs. Of course, he should also let him understand its "reason for being." I believe that the author of "Bacteria and Humans" can, with his talent, write out the mysterious and wonderful scientific truth "in simple and interesting words."

Perhaps inadvertently, the author sometimes reveals some traditional consciousness in his writing. For example, what "Heaven... has the virtue of loving life." Such words should not be found in the works of a scientist. We know that the changes in nature are carried out according to the laws of nature, and are definitely not governed by any "Heaven." I hope the author will be more careful when writing in the future.

Some people say that although some of the articles in this collection are very beautiful, the literary content they contain seems to be too strong and not understandable by the general public. This is an undeniable fact. However, regarding this point, the author has already corrected it in his recently published articles.

In summary, on the whole, "Bacteria and Humans" is a very excellent collection of scientific essays, and it is a good book worth reading.
